The IDA is a supramolecular sensing strategy where a receptor binds an indicator molecule, and the presence of the target analyte (here, quinine) displaces the indicator, leading to a measurable signal change. While IDA is most commonly associated with optical (colorimetric or fluorescent) detection, recent advances have extended its application to electrochemical sensors. In such systems, the displacement of an electroactive indicator by quinine can be monitored via changes in current, potential, or impedance at the sensor electrode. The choice of receptor is critical: it must selectively bind both the indicator and quinine, with a higher affinity for quinine. Calixarene derivatives, for example, have been used as supramolecular hosts in IDA-based sensors and could be tailored for quinine recognition. The electrochemical readout provides advantages such as high sensitivity, miniaturization, and suitability for point-of-care testing. While literature examples of IDA-based electrochemical sensors for quinine are limited compared to optical methods, the approach is feasible and aligns with current trends in supramolecular The IDA is a supramolecular sensing strategy where a receptor binds an indicator molecule, and the presence of the target analyte (here, quinine) displaces the indicator, leading to a measurable signal change. While IDA is most commonly associated with optical (colorimetric or fluorescent) detection, recent advances have extended its application to electrochemical sensors. In such systems, the displacement of an electroactive indicator by quinine can be monitored via changes in current, potential, or impedance at the sensor electrode. The choice of receptor is critical: it must selectively bind both the indicator and quinine, with a higher affinity for quinine. Calixarene derivatives, for example, have been used as supramolecular hosts in IDA-based sensors and could be tailored for quinine recognition. The electrochemical readout provides advantages such as high sensitivity, miniaturization, and suitability for point-of-care testing. While literature examples of IDA-based electrochemical sensors for quinine are limited compared to optical methods, the approach is feasible and aligns with current trends in supramolecular The IDA is a supramolecular sensing strategy where a receptor binds an indicator molecule, and the presence of the target analyte (here, quinine) displaces the indicator, leading to a measurable signal change. While IDA is most commonly associated with optical (colorimetric or fluorescent) detection, recent advances have extended its application to electrochemical sensors. In such systems, the displacement of an electroactive indicator by quinine can be monitored via changes in current, potential, or impedance at the sensor electrode. The choice of receptor is critical: it must selectively bind both the indicator and quinine, with a higher affinity for quinine. Calixarene derivatives, for example, have been used as supramolecular hosts in IDA-based sensors and could be tailored for quinine recognition. The electrochemical readout provides advantages such as high sensitivity, miniaturization, and suitability for point-of-care testing. The IDA is a supramolecular sensing strategy where a receptor binds an indicator molecule, and the presence of the target analyte (here, quinine) displaces the indicator, leading to a measurable signal change. While IDA is most commonly associated with optical (colorimetric or fluorescent) detection, recent advances have extended its application to electrochemical sensors. In such systems, the displacement of an electroactive indicator by quinine can be monitored via changes in current, potential, or impedance at the sensor electrode. The choice of receptor is critical: it must selectively bind both the indicator and quinine, with a higher affinity for quinine. Calixarene derivatives, for example, have been used as supramolecular hosts in IDA-based sensors and could be tailored for quinine recognition. The electrochemical readout provides advantages such as high sensitivity, miniaturization, and suitability for point-of-care testing. While literature examples of IDA-based electrochemical sensors for quinine are limited compared to optical methods, the approach is feasible and aligns with current trends in supramolecular
